New York Auto Show by Ping Baoshan 204

Opening on January 3rd

Monday, January 5th, is designated as International Day. The 31st annual auto show, held at the Grand Central Palace in New York from January 3rd to 10th, 1931, will extend hospitality to overseas guests. The planned program is highly valuable and will ensure that attendees' visit is worthwhile.

On the morning of the 5th, a special conference will be held for International Day, providing guests with an opportunity to meet with prominent figures from various automobile factories. Issues such as the global distribution and operation of automobile manufacturing, as well as recent developments in the automobile industry and transportation, will be discussed one by one. In the afternoon, overseas guests can visit the auto show at the Grand Central Palace, where samples of automobiles and related products from 360 manufacturers will be displayed and distributed. Detailed answers will be provided to any questions.

On the evening of Tuesday, January 6th, all guests are invited to the annual banquet at the Commodore Hotel, where leaders in the domestic automobile industry will gather.

During the following week, anyone interested can form groups to visit and inspect automobile factories in Detroit and neighboring areas.

The New York headquarters, located at 366 Madison Avenue, welcomes guests to register and collect exhibition badges on the opening day of the exhibition (Saturday, January 3rd).

The paint color of the car body uses the Tucker blending method, making it bright and radiant, and even more magnificent and luxurious than the 1930 model.

The radiator is one of the most important components of a car, as the engine can overheat after prolonged driving. This is entirely reliant on the radiator to dissipate the heat. The new 1931 car has paid special attention to this, increasing the size of the radiator's cold water pipe to prevent damage to the cylinders. The placement of the horn is even more unique, being installed directly below the left headlight, making it beautiful and unconventional in appearance and extremely convenient to use without taking up space. In addition to these, a mudguard has been newly invented. Cars driving on muddy or rainy streets often become covered in mud and unsightly stains. Now, with the installation of this device, cars can avoid this problem and remain clean all year round. This is another unique feature of the car.

The car body is manufactured by the famous Fisher Body Company. The current trend in the automotive industry is towards low-profile car bodies, and the new car meets this requirement. Therefore, it looks refined and lovely, indistinguishable from high-priced cars. The car body painting is extremely practical and provides unlimited convenience for drivers.

Speaking of the car's interior, because the wheelbase has been increased by two inches, the space is exceptionally spacious. The layout includes ash receptacles, armrests, silk woven cushions, and shatterproof glass to prevent danger. Although these features have existed in the past, their application to cars priced below $490 is a first. All metal parts inside the car are chrome-plated, and doors can be opened with a light touch, without requiring much effort. The driver's seat has also been greatly improved, and all tools are systematically arranged, making them easy to use at a glance. Other components also contribute significantly.

Development of British-Made Motorcycles 205

Possessing the Advantages of Small Cars

In recent years, the British automobile manufacturing industry has seen the emergence of so-called "baby" cars, which have become quite popular. Initially, only one or two manufacturers created these extremely small cars, but now even famous automobile factories are producing this style of car, and their designs are extremely exquisite. These so-called small cars are highly efficient, low in price, and very economical to operate. In short, the success of these small cars has led many to speculate that they would completely eliminate motorcycles.

Fortunately, this fear has not materialized. The number of motorcycle buyers remains very large, and British manufacturers are making every effort to improve motorcycle manufacturing. As a result, not only are their products the best in the world, but they also enjoy a significant advantage in sales. This situation also applies to the bicycle manufacturing industry.

The year 1929 was a record year for British bicycles and motorcycles. In terms of motorcycles alone, British-made motorcycles accounted for 60% of the global output. This year, however, British motorcycle production is likely to decrease. In the first six months, exports fell from 42,798 units last year to 30,921 units this year. However, overall, the percentage of British exports is likely to remain close to that of 1929, as the decline in production is common to other countries as well. For example, the United States' production in the first six months of this year compared to the same period last year has fallen by the same amount as Britain, accounting for 28%. The reason for the decline in production is probably due to the decline of various industries, but the situation varies from country to country. The economic crisis in Australia has affected British motorcycle exports, accounting for 25% in the first six months of this year.

In the first six months of 1929, bicycle exports amounted to 190,523 units, while the number for the same period this year was 152,216 units, a decrease of about 20%. Of this decline, 75% is due to the unrest in India, which purchased 27,000 fewer British bicycles than last year. The Chinese market has also been severely affected by the political situation, resulting in sluggish sales of British bicycles, which has nothing to do with the quality of the products.

The annual motorcycle and bicycle exhibition in Olympia fully demonstrates the level of British-made products. This year's products are indeed superior to those of previous years. A total of 46 motorcycle manufacturers participated in the exhibition, displaying 160 models, of which only five were foreign products. The improvements in style and construction are noteworthy. Five years ago, the average motorcycle cost about 120 pounds, but now a motorcycle with the speed and comfort of a small car costs only three-quarters of that.

The motorcycles on display at this year's exhibition range from those priced at 20 pounds to more luxurious models. The cars feature various speed mechanisms, three-wheeled configurations, and comfortable weather adjustments, while remaining extremely economical to operate. Although there are no shocking innovations, the overall progress is evident. British manufacturers have also made excellent progress in eliminating obnoxious noises. There have been many improvements in the engine, making it cleaner and lighter, with balanced power, easy starting, and improved gear shifting. The automatic oiling system is also very effective.

The historical motorcycles displayed by the Royal Automobile Club are most likely to attract the attention of visitors. These groundbreaking motorcycles not only illustrate various advances in style but also have other fascinating origins. For example, one car has a frame made entirely of wood, another is propelled by steam, and yet another has its components completely installed in the rear wheel. There are also two sidecars that have carried their owners around the world. Overall, the exhibition was a success, and even foreign visitors highly praised the British-made motorcycles.

Book and Newspaper Introductions

Sister's Remains by Wu Xiaotian 206

Published by Caoye Society

This is a recently published collection of essays. The author, Tang Zengyang, is known for his poetry. I remember reading his poetry collection, "A Solitary Song," a year ago. In each of his lyrical poems, he could stir my inner emotions because the true emotions expressed in poetry are the most genuine aspect of life, especially since poets possess extremely keen senses. Therefore, people in the literary world often say that writing essays with a poet's pen is the most beautiful and interesting. However, the "Sister's Remains" collection contains even more of these beautiful elements, making it easier for us to be moved. It is truly like singing a beautiful lyrical poem.

New literature in China seems to be showing signs of decline, especially in the nineteenth year of the Republic. We only need to look at the literary books presented to the reading public by the domestic publishing industry, which are as rare as morning stars. The categories of poetry and prose have almost fallen into silence, even though some such works are occasionally published in various publications. However, their power is very small and cannot attract special attention from readers. This is a very obvious fact. Since the publication of Tang's "Sister's Remains," it has broken the silence in the literary world. It seems to have considerable power, as this collection of essays suddenly appears before the reader's eyes, which is truly unexpected. However, the essence of the content in this collection certainly has the value of being published. This crystallization of blood and tears expresses the author's unique greatness everywhere. Regarding this point, Mr. Sun Dayong's preface analyzes it in great detail. The preface begins like this:

In modern China, filled with terrifying murderous intent and with the river of love dried up, how delighted I am to read this collection by Tang Zengyang, "Sister's Remains."

The world is too big, and human reason is so complex and contradictory that the meaning of life disappears invisibly in this phenomenon, which is truly not worthwhile.

Love is the net that connects the complexity and contradictions of human reason. Poets are the ones who hold tightly to the total network, the top of the net, and spread the meshes of the net so that the earth and humanity are enveloped beneath it. Everything on earth, everything human, whether tangible or intangible, is purified, harmonized, beautified, and reaches the realm of the greatness of life. This is the poet's skill.

What Zengyang expresses in this collection is filled with that thing called love. His "sister" is nothing more than a symbol of love, and her "remains" are precisely the image of love's being ravaged, trampled, and being the expression of love's river dried up after experiencing terrifying murderous intent.

This is truly the poet's painstaking effort.

Here, Mr. Sun has already explained the meaning contained in this collection of essays with exceptional detail. However, this is by no means an ordinary and unfounded explanation. Undoubtedly, it is based on the principles of poetry, paying more attention to the unique expression of love by the author in this collection of essays. Therefore, we will be deeply convinced of the phrase (the poet's emotions are especially rich) after reading this collection. At the same time, we must understand that not long ago, the Chinese literary scene was not only a scene of utter darkness but also filled with extreme terrifying murderous intent. The so-called proletarian poetry, besides barking for practical purposes, at most, was only waving flags and shouting slogans, without any meaning at all. The forms and techniques were not given any attention, making us feel that it was merely a literary work composed of slogans and banners, which simply could not be. This phenomenon was certainly the greatest crisis presented by the Chinese literary world. This (Sister's Remains) is a successful work in terms of poetic essence and has reached the highest peak of artistic defense.

The entire book contains eighteen essays: (1) Moonlit Night, (2) Lingering Ghost, (3) Shadow of Tears, (4) Sister's Remains, (5) Her Cry, (6) Winter's Bitter Smile, (7) Blessing, (8) Red Wine of Life, (9) Skull in the Graveyard Grass, (10) It's Late at Night, (11) Fate of Fallen Flowers, (12) Vague Destination, (13) Destruction of Love, (14) Nameless Poetic Soul, (15) New Baby, (16) Disabled Soul, (17) Fossil of the Ancient Sea, (18) Last Funeral Bell. The volume begins with a prefatory poem (the prophecy before death is especially gentle and fluent and is truly a rare poem).

(The poet's sadness is probably his nature. This is something everyone admits. Zengyang's collection has a heavy color of sadness, which is also natural.) This is what Mr. Sun said in the preface, but it has a full reason. For example, the author says this in the (Moonlit Night) essay:

(In the flower shade covered by the bright moon, I, who have fallen into the sea of suffering, once embraced a young girl and gave her the comfort of love. The soil still remains on her haggard lips, and the lipstick that is about to fade. Her tired and shrunken ears are still full of low, mournful songs, and the sound waves of delicate gasps are deeply imprinted in my lonely mind. In fact, the past illusions unfold like a silver screen. In the midst of a tragic and blurred recall, I cannot help but feel disheartened. Now, I have bid farewell to my lovely hometown and sunk into the abyss of a foreign land.

Gazing at the clouds and mountains, I can only shed tears.)

(Here, the poet has already nakedly displayed a sad heart before people.) (However, the sadness is true sadness, not a vain, moaning sadness. Therefore, Zengyang's sadness has its own characteristics. He gently stepped over his "Sister's Remains" and walked to the world of mankind. Therefore, his sadness also has such... He has already transcended the real situation of sadness and stepped into the ideal country.) This is written very clearly in his (Lingering Ghost), but his sadness contains infinite effort and hope. The author clearly writes: (Happiness requires courage to pursue, and happiness requires effort to move forward. Historical facts tell me this. I feel....) The past has become an elusive illusion, and the future may contain a ray of brilliant hope. From now on, I can only muster my inherent energy and fight forward against the darkness that hinders the free progress of mankind, and seize the glory that shines in the front of the era... (Moonlit Night) (In the hazy thoughts, I only seek a bright future.) (Winter's Bitter Smile)

These descriptions are all different from those of decadent poets who only focus on sadness. Truly, like what is written in (Winter's Bitter Smile), (how to work hard is even more greatness that is not easy for ordinary poets to have). This is truly the most correct and inevitable criticism. (As for the precise refinement of words, the detailed description, and the neat structure in the collection,) I also agree with what Mr. Sun said (everyone who reads it wants to feel it, so I won't describe it).

International Trade Herald

Since the Shanghai Commodity Inspection Bureau issued the International Trade Herald, the materials have been rich and sales have increased, especially among Japanese buyers. It has now been published to the ninth issue of the first volume (December issue). Important texts include articles on "Export Merchants Should Voluntarily Organize Export Trade Associations" (Jing Wei), "The Problem of Cornering and Hoarding in International Trade" (Hou Houpei), "Eliminating Foreign Enterprises in China to Resist Economic Aggression" (Zhu Binyuan), "The Situation of World Sugar Production and the Sugar Industry" (Cheng Tianshou), "China's Sugar Imports and Consumption over the Years" (Rao Xinmei), "Japan's Pyrethrum Industry and Its Position in International Trade" (Wu Deming), and "The System and Management of Japanese Silk Sales Cooperatives" (Huang Heqing). It is said that the next issue is scheduled to be published on January 1, 2020, as the first issue of the second volume.

Since its publication, Pioneer Weekly has been a pioneering literary publication that first advocated nationalism. The twenty-third issue was published the day before yesterday. The main contents include Xiang Hua's (On Nationalist Drama) (continued), Zhang Jiping's (Review of the Nationalist Literary Movement), Xiao Jia's (The Fall of the Mountain Country), Jin Xuan's Tanfeng (Literati and Poverty), and Zheng Bo's (Two Orphans), etc. The Modern, Daguang, and various school gatehouses in this city all have sales outlets, and major bookstores in other cities have distributors. Each copy costs three copper coins.

Caoye Weekly, Volume 4, No. 2, was published the day before yesterday and was scheduled to be sold the day before yesterday (Monday). The content is rich, and in the future, it will be sold every Saturday without delay. Readers are requested to pay attention. This issue is already available for sale at various newspaper vendors and school gatehouses.

Guidelines for Book and Newspaper Introduction Manuscripts

(1) Texts are limited to introducing the latest domestic and foreign publications. In addition to literary works, all books and newspapers on politics, economics, law, science, education, etc., are included in the introduction. However, books and newspapers with no connection will not be introduced.

(2) Pictures are limited to recent works by new domestic and foreign artists and related illustrations to the text.

(3) Texts on textual research or research on ancient Chinese books will not be published.

(4) Pictures and photos are limited to those related to the text.

(5) Each text should not exceed 3,000 words at most.

(6) Do not write on both sides of the manuscript paper.

(7) The manuscript should not be written horizontally, and the words should not be cursive. Western texts can be omitted if possible. If it is necessary to quote the original text, it must be attached below the translation.

Art World

Japanese Imperial Art Exhibition of Western Paintings 207

Xie Haiyan, Japanese Correspondent

(Continued from the Fifth)

Looking at Mr. Sang Chongyi's "Chinese Twin Beauties," I cannot help but think of Mr. Zheng Mantuo, the Shanghai calendar poster artist.

Mr. Tanabe Itaru's "Studio" is purely a product of the official academic school, with many shortcomings. The main ones are that the sensuality of the nude is too strong, the expression of spatiality is too shallow, and it is too focused on detailed depiction.

Mr. Ikebe Jun's "Dance" is by an author who was originally a renowned cartoonist. Using his accustomed cartooning techniques to create this dance of figures naturally achieves considerable effect.

Mr. Nagahara Kotaro's "Star" is not of the twentieth century and therefore has no relation to us. The author is a surviving member of the White Horse Society.

Mr. Okubo Sakujiro's "Vegetable Market" is commonplace.

Mr. Ishikawa Toraji's "Spring in Suzhou" is dry, and Mr. Kinzan Heizo's "Spring" is delicate, with the style of Wada.

Mr. Takama Sochichi's "Morning at the Window" has an unbalanced composition and lacks a unified effect, but its light and bright colors are enough to redeem its lost appeal. His style has much in common with Mr. Makino's, but Mr. Makino excels in his vigorous and unrestrained spirit, while he excels in his light and elegant spirit.

A Chinese-style hall with an ancient and elegant atmosphere is arranged with a folding chair and a small tea table. On the table is a vase of blooming fresh flowers, and behind the flowers hangs a couplet in clerical script with seven characters each. In the right corner hangs a red gauze lantern, and under the lantern is a girl in a blue floral satin dress, extending her fair hand as if lighting the lamp. This painting is titled "China Lantern" and the author is Mr. Takamura Masao, a surviving member of the Bu-She school and a member of the current Pacific Painting Society. I really cannot understand why this painting was not hung together with Mr. Wada Eisaku's "Early Spring."

Mr. Mita Yasushi's "Seated Portrait" is a work with a cautious attitude, with an excellent sense of color.

Mr. Suzuki Chikumasa's "Nude on Grass" is a major achievement of this year's Imperial Exhibition. Its strong and dominant presence is enough to overwhelm the generally timid artists of the Imperial Exhibition. The painting is very large, at least 300 size or more. Every year's Imperial Exhibition, especially this year's, has countless paintings of this size, all for the sake of fame (naturally for vanity) and prize money. It is no wonder they want to do so, although Mr. Suzuki is not merely doing it for this reason.

Mr. Ihara Usaburo's "Two Figures" forms a triangle with two nude women, complemented by contrasting colors of green, blue, and red, possessing a kind of elegant and skillful technique. Mr. Ihara's style was originally somewhat similar to Picasso's, but this year's work is far from it. A clear flaw is that the entire painting has fallen into ordinary realism, gradually tending towards the official academic school. Mr. Ihara is currently ill, probably due to the exhaustion of his efforts for this difficult birth of an infant. I add this note here to wish him a speedy recovery.

Mr. Asai Makoto's "White Ship Entering Port" gives me infinite interest and comfort, as if I have returned to Shanghai, standing on the railing of the Bund Park by the Huangpu River. Everyone knows the famous goat painter Mr. Tsuji Hisashi of Japan, but it is a great pity that he has long been tired of being given this name and has not used goats as a subject for a long time. He exhibited a landscape painting titled "Garden" at this year's Imperial Exhibition. Although I do not have the good fortune to see the famous goats of the goat painter, I can still see a bit of the goat's interest in this small landscape painting.

Mr. Nakamura Fusetsu, a member of the Imperial Art Academy and the leader of the Pacific Painting Society, is a champion of the official academic school and the only writer of conceptual paintings, firmly defending his stubborn fortress and resisting the trends of the new era. People who worry about him say, "This old man always produces a Chinese historical painting at every exhibition, such as 'Lu Sheng's Dream,' 'Du Shu's Well,' 'Roasting Taro Without Answering the Envoy,' and 'Earning the Lanting Preface.' This year he produced another one called 'Su Wu's Loyalty.' At this rate, will there ever be an end to Chinese historical stories?" In fact, those who worry about him are short-sighted. China is the oldest country with thousands of years of cultural history. Why worry about not having strange historical subjects to supply him with? If he can paint fifty paintings every year, and if he can live to be five hundred years old, I think there will always be subjects to supply him with.

Mr. Inokuma Ichiro's "Composition" is a masterpiece that stands out in the field, with the compositional interest of Picasso and Derain.

In this turbid muddy pool, a large, noble white lotus grows, making people feel particularly lovely. This is Mr. Nakano and Mr. Takako's "Untitled," a masterpiece depicting Japanese rural life. The whole painting is filled with the music of lyrical poetry, and the low-key mood is a victory achieved by exploring the free world with a modern sensibility. The composition is very interesting. The horses and figures on the left, as well as the houses and trees in the background, form a small group. The standing figure and the reclining figure on the right form a large group. Then, the mountains and forests in the background and the three wooden poles in the front are connected to form a very harmonious and unified picture. Finally, the life of this masterpiece is completed with free and unrestrained brushstrokes and simple and lively colors.

Mr. Saito Yori's "Spring Evening" is a pastoral scene of a spring evening, but unfortunately its purpose is closer to a story and further from reality.

Mr. Yoshimura Yoshimatsu's "Recluse in Season" is a microcosm of an old Chinese family.

Mr. Sasaki Tokujiro's "Rocks" is too mired in realism, and Mr. Uenosan Seikyo's "Navigation" has too strong a load-bearing capacity of muddy colors, and the composition is childish and lacks integration. It is an unfinished work.

Other works include Mr. Kataoka Ginzo's "Reclining Nude," Mr. Yajima Kent's "Interior," Mr. Terauchi Manjiro's "Nude," Mr. Kakizaki Moichiro's "Sounkyo in Hokkaido," Mr. Arai Kan's "Tenpyo Statue," Mr. Ataka Angoro's "Hair," Mr. Hattori Ryohei's "Nude," Mr. Matsumura Tatsumi's "By the Window," Mr. Kumaoka Yoshihiko's "Scarlet Collection," Mr. Yoshida Hiroshi's "Sea of Clouds," Mr. Shimizu Yoshio's "Setting Sun," Mr. Koshiba Kinji's "Les Dames Aux Roses," Mr. Mikami Tomoharu's "Wolfhound," Mr. Shirakawa Ikunosuke's "Autumn Suburbs," Mr. Nagachi Shutai's "Wheat Field," Mr. Yunoki Kyuta's "Fountain," Mr. Arima Satoe's "Person Sketching," Mr. Kotera Kenkichi's "Thin Nude," Mr. Ono Takanori's "Cradle and Child," etc. Among these works, there are also good ones, but due to time constraints, I cannot introduce them one by one, which is really a pity. If there is an opportunity in the future, I will bring them up again and discuss them with you in detail. Now, this tour of the Western Painting Section of the Imperial Exhibition comes to an end here.

New Trends in American Literary Circles 208

(Continued from the Third) Translated by Anping

Therefore, in this light, it is not surprising that American writers praise and admire England, just as DOWNING STREET (magazine name) discusses American finance, and London magazines discuss the standards and decisions of today's critics. If an American writer is recognized by England, he will immediately become an international celebrity. He not only gains his reputation, but also gains something in tangible terms. In any case, he always gets the glory. Naturally, this part of the relationship is entirely psychologically influenced, but their reputation is still undeniable. Only those writers who write for another kind of reader, such as the American pioneers Whitman and MacThane, who have a soul about national borders, only those writers can not be influenced by that British air. However, since the international status of the United States in the world has risen, this influence has gradually decreased. In the past, American literature did not have its own status, because the United States did not have its own full status among the Western countries. It was a growing country, but it was not yet fully grown. As soon as the international status of the United States changed, this situation immediately changed, and the psychology of the country also changed. Gradually, American writers felt that American literature must have its own place and should be connected with its (country). At such a time, Whitman and MacThane were recognized by those literary artists and critics. Perhaps if the United States had not joined the World War and had not taken over the leadership of the world from Britain, this transformation would still not have been successful. But in any case, the British air left to the United States has at least reached its end. From that time on, American literature, filled with a new force, was liberated from the old shackles. He has deep faith in his own talent and does not feel that there is anything more praiseworthy than carving out their new destiny.

What is the nature of this new destiny? Who are the representatives of this new wave? In the aspect of poetry, we see them in what they call the (literary radical hall). They represent this new atmosphere in their own special style. Those poets such as Lowell (AMY LOWELL), Frost (ROBERT FIOST), Sandburg (CARL SANDEERG), Lindsay (VACHELL LINDSAY), Jeffers (ROBINSON JEFFERS), and Masters (EDGAR MEE MASTERS) are all writers of this kind. But these writers, whether directly or indirectly, are all influenced by Whitman's poetry to some extent. No one can transfer the names of British model writers to them as a kind of praise. They are all very obviously and completely American. Even Lowell, although he is deeply influenced by the British atmosphere, his emotions still do not escape the memories of Montresor and roses, but he also joined the anti-British rebellion like a MIDWESTERN poet. In fact, her enthusiastic advocacy of the new trend in modern poetry has not been of much help and strength to the revival of American poetry. In the same situation as Lowell are two women, one named Monroe (HARRIER MONROE), who gathered a large group of rebellious poets of this era in her magazine "Poetry," and the other named Anderson (MARGARET ANDERSON), who also worked hard in her "Little Magazine" (THE LITTLE REVIEW) in the same way as Monroe, but what she worked hard on was not poetry but prose.

But these poets only expressed the American background in their personal will. Their actions are completely personal and not social. This is the reason why the challenger of Whitman's old poetry style can overcome the new school. As in the poems of Cummings (EECUMMINGS) and Stein (GEITRIDE SEIN), individualism is revealed so strongly that it almost completely becomes a desperate exorcism symbol (ABRACADABRA). But in any case, on the whole, the poems of Sandburg, Frost, Robinson, Lindsay, and Masters all contain a balance of external and internal elements. According to the American situation, these new poets who show contempt for their personal conflicts in theory and technique unanimously oppose Howell's words ("The more obvious and active the spirit is in life, the more it can be said to be an American"). They feel that what is presented in the United States now is not a bit of ("active spirit of life"), but a bit of expansion of land. The United States is now becoming the leading country in the world, a global power. But in what point can he show his power? On the machine, in the invention, in the goods, a country revolving on the axis of steam and steel. But what things have been encountered in the individual's soul? He will be destroyed when he will be destroyed in the preliminary proceedings, and the spirit of this individualism in the literary rebellion will eventually be sacrificed and tend to the aspect of the group's function.
